It would fit better with the Pride issue’s bigger message.” Agatha looks at her so Rio continues. “Right now it feels like a listicle, but if the angle is more about identity and conscious fashion, it could feel like a real movement. Not just a shopping guide.”

Agatha makes a thoughtful sound, her eyes returning to the spread as if reevaluating it through Rio’s suggestion. “Okay. If we tighten the intro and cut some of the fluff, it might actually ground the culture section better than what we had before,” she says, then adds, almost offhand, “Hmm. That could work.”

And Rio nods like that didn’t just set off a small firework in her chest.

“Your professors must’ve either adored you or wanted to strangle you.”

Rio chuckles. “Most of them actually liked me. I toe the line just enough to be endearing.”

They stand there a little longer, going over the other options side by side. Rio leans in to point at something on the page, and Agatha moves closer to get a better look. Suddenly, they’re almost shoulder to shoulder, heads tilted toward the same spot. That’s when Rio catches the soft scent of Agatha’s lavender perfume. She looks up without meaning to, only to realize Agatha’s already looking at her. 

And for one dizzy second, Rio’s pretty sure she sees her blue eyes drop down to her lips.

Rio tells herself not to read into it. Not to freeze. Not to stare. But something in her doesn’t listen. Because all she can think about now is how close they are. How Agatha’s lips look even softer up close, just barely parted, like she’s about to speak or forgot how to. And somehow, in the middle of all that thinking: Rio realizes, really realizes, how badly she wants to know what it feels like to kiss her. What it would be like to make someone like Agatha lose control, even just once. 

And before she can stop herself, she leans in just enough to cross that thin line between looking and wanting. Her breath catches. She doesn’t even know what she’s doing anymore. It’s like something else is taking over. And to her surprise, Agatha doesn’t even move away.

And then…

A knock at the open door.

Both of them flinch at the same time, like teenagers caught doing something they shouldn’t. Rio straightens up so fast she almost knocks the papers off the desk, while Agatha clears her throat and turns away, her expression snapping back into something more neutral and composed, but also a bit pissed off. 

Rio recognizes the face as one of the new junior editors. He’s poking his head through the glass door like he’s not entirely sure he should be doing this. 

“Hey, sorry to interrupt,” he says, and Rio can already tell that he’s probably never spoken to Agatha before. Maybe someone from layout dared him to be the messenger. “Just wanted to give you a heads-up. The book might be a little delayed tonight. One of the printers downstairs is acting up. We’ve already called maintenance, but—” 

Agatha doesn’t even look up from the spread. 

“And what exactly do you expect me to do with that information?”

Rio watches him instantly freeze in the doorway.

“I—I just thought you should know.”

Rio hears Agatha let out a dry, humorless laugh. The kind that doesn’t mean she’s amused at all. She shakes her head slowly like she’s trying to hold onto her patience by a thread. 

“Why?” she says, finally looking up at him. “Did you honestly think I’d be thrilled to hear that? Or were you hoping I’d roll up my sleeves and fix the printer myself?” 

The whole room goes still, and Rio watches the poor junior editor shrink two inches on the spot. He opens his mouth, then clearly thinks better of whatever he was going to say.

“What’s your name?” Agatha asks. 

“Um. Dylan.”

Poor Dylan, Rio thinks, wincing a little on his behalf.

“Well, Dylan,” Agatha says, drawing out his name like it leaves a bad taste in her mouth. “Let me make something very clear. If you’re going to knock on my door, don’t walk in here just to hand me a problem, unless it’s a problem that’s already been fixed. Otherwise, don’t come in at all. Am I making myself clear?”

Dylan nods quickly. “Yes, absolutely. Sorry. I’ll, uh, I’ll just go now,” he stammers, turning on his heel so fast and practically speed-walking. Rio’s sure she hears a tiny whimper as he disappears down the hall. 

Agatha exhales through her nose, pinching the bridge of it. “Why is basic competence so rare?” she grunts, more to herself than to Rio, before walking over to her other desk and sinking into her chair. 

Rio doesn’t say anything. She just stands there, still stuck on what almost happened before someone walked in and cut it short. Her mind keeps replaying it, trying to make sense of what just happened. She swears she didn’t imagine it. Agatha didn’t pull away. She didn’t even stop her. She was leaning in too. Right? Or is Rio just sleep-deprived and starting to lose her mind?

Before the silence can stretch too long and turn weird, Agatha clears her throat. 

“I’ll take the book with me tonight. You don’t have to wait around for it.”

“Are you sure? I really don’t mind waiting—”

“You’ve done enough,” Agatha says, her tone a little too close to the one she used on Dylan just seconds ago. And even though Rio tells herself not to take it personally, it still stings. “You’ve had a long day,” Agatha adds. “Just go home. Get some rest.”

Rio hesitates, still feeling something humming under her skin from that almost-moment. But she lets it go. “Alright,” she says carefully. “Goodnight, Agatha.”

Agatha doesn’t even look up. Doesn’t say anything back. Just stays hunched over her desk, flipping a page like nothing happened at all.

“What if I just quit?” Rio calls out from the bathroom, a toothbrush hanging from her mouth.

Alice, somewhere in the kitchen probably making her usual peanut butter and jelly sandwich, yells back, “Honestly? I’d say it’s about damn time.” Then there’s the unmistakable clink of a butter knife against a jar. “But didn’t you say there’s been some progress with you, like, finally speaking up at meetings and having opinions and whatever?” 

Rio spits, wipes her mouth on a towel, then pads barefoot into the dining room in an old NYU hoodie and a pair of boxers that definitely shouldn’t count as pants. “Yeah, I was. I mean, I am. But I don’t know. I don’t think I like it there anymore. Lately, it’s been hard to focus. Too many distractions.”

Alice pokes her head out from the kitchen, peanut butter knife in hand. “Distractions?” she repeats, suddenly very interested. “What kind of distractions?” Rio says nothing as Alice narrows her eyes, then her jaw drops. “Holy shit. You slept with your boss. You slept with her, didn’t you?” 

Rio’s mouth falls open. “Jesus, no! I didn’t sleep with anyone!”

“Is that why you came home so late the other night?”

“No!” Rio groans and covers her face. “No, Alice. I didn’t sleep with my fucking boss!”

Alice hurriedly walks into the dining room and drops onto the chair across from her. 

“But you want to. You’ve been thinking about it.”

Rio doesn't answer.

“Oh my god. You want to get into her designer pants. I knew it! You’ve had that look on your face for days every time her name comes up. You want to climb that terrifying woman like she’s a jungle gym.”

Rio glares at her. “Alice, do you even hear yourself right now?”

Alice just grins. 

“Look, I get it. I mean, I was a little worried natural brunettes might not be her type, but you’ve got a thing. Kinda weird, kinda intense. Most girls dig that. And you’re hot. Very hot. And charming. And smart. She’d be out of her mind not to want you. And you’d be even hotter if you went blonde again, by the way.”

Rio hides her face again, but the pink creeping into her cheeks gives her away. 

“Alice, can you not?” she grumbles. “And you’re right. I’m not her type.”

“But you like her.” Alice smirks. “Admit it. You’ve always had a thing for older women.”

“That’s not true.”

“Oh yeah? What about the adjunct professor from junior year with the motorcycle? The 40-year-old tattoo artist who drove a hearse? And that divorcee from Brooklyn who made you vegan brownies and cried so hard when her cat died?” 

“Okay, okay, stop. Fine. Maybe I like her. A little. But that doesn’t mean I’m going to sleep with her. There is no sleeping. There is no sex. Zero. None. Absolutely no boss-fucking.”

“Who’s not fucking their boss?” Billy announces as he walks through the open door 

“Jesus, kid. Ever heard of knocking?” Rio groans.

“Sorry. But your door’s open,” he shrugs, then steps back out just to knock on it. Rio flips him off as he strolls to the table and flops into the chair beside Alice. “So. You’re not doing your hot boss from Pride?”

“I swear to God, I’m gonna strangle both of you,” Rio hisses. “And no, I’m not doing my boss. And stop calling her hot. And can you not insert yourself into grown-up conversations?”

“I’m seventeen. I’m legally allowed to have opinions on sex,” Billy argues.

“And apparently, none on volume control,” Rio retorts.

Billy lunges forward and grabs the sandwich Alice hands him.

“So, if you’re not sleeping with her, maybe it’s because you don’t exactly look like someone she’d want to sleep with. No offense. You’re definitely hot. Even my lesbian friends say so.” He takes a bite, completely unbothered, and Rio instantly regrets showing up to one of his school plays last month. “Hot in a scary, slightly threatening sense. But if you want her to see you differently, you’ve got to look the part.”

“I don’t want her to want me, okay?” Rio says through gritted teeth. “I don’t even have the part.” 

“I think you do,” Billy insists, dead serious. How old is this kid again? “And I can help. Alice and I can help. You’ve got the raw materials. Cheekbones, bone structure, and all that. But you dress like you’re Shane’s mechanic in that lesbian show my mom won’t shut up about. Clothes matter, okay? Trust me.”

“The kid has a point.” Alice sips her juice. “About the wardrobe, I mean. No offense. You know I love you.” 

“And I fucking hate you right now,” Rio says, squinting at her. Then she turns to Billy. “And don’t you have better things to do? Like wash your mom’s car, text that guy you keep pretending you’re not into, or livestream yourself ranking every Taylor Swift bridge in chronological order?”

“Did all three earlier,” Billy shrugs. “I’m free now. So if you want, we can go out, check a few places. Just see what we can find. Give your closet a little…glow-up or something.”

Rio’s eyebrows shoot up. 

“Absolutely not. There is no way I’m letting a seventeen-year-old play dress-up with me. I’d rather chew glass. I will rip out my own eyebrows with pliers before I let either of you give me a makeover.”

But a few hours later, Rio walks out of the salon with fresh layers and curtain bangs she never asked for, but secretly kind of loves. She’s got three thrift store bags hanging from each hand, while Alice and Billy trail behind her, grinning, arms full of their own haul.

Weirdly enough, Billy and Alice forcing a makeover down Rio’s throat the day before turns out to be freakishly well-timed. Agatha, apparently still in Miami but will be flying back shortly, had called Jen to say that Rio will be tagging along to the annual benefit tonight, helping Jen make sure every guest on the list would think Agatha knows exactly who they are.

Billy, of course, had treated it like he’d been training his whole life for this, practically shoving Rio into a midnight blue suit, tailored fit and a plunging neckline, like some dangerously hot, gender-bent version of Cinderella on her one shot at the ball.

Billy had said she looked great. Alice had said she’d turn heads. And that’s what Rio keeps repeating to herself as she steps out of the car and walks toward the venue. Jen’s already by the bar, champagne flute in hand, clearly waiting for their boss. She gives Rio a once-over and lifts her glass, “You clean up alright.”

They stay like that for a while, keeping their drinks slow, just sober enough not to lose their jobs, while watching the crowd swell with stylists, models, writers, sponsors, execs, and even interns. 

The lights get warmer, the music louder, and half an hour goes by. Then, like a flip of a switch, heads suddenly turn toward the entrance, and a swarm of photographers rushes toward the doors in a whirl of shutters and shouts of “Miss Harkness! This way, Miss Harkness!”

Agatha walks in wearing a black satin gown, her hair falling in soft waves, lips painted a sharp, perfect red. She smiles for the cameras, greets a few guests, and takes photos with some. 

All eyes are on Agatha, but hers ultimately settle on just one. Blue meets brown, and for a second, she slips. As if she forgets to hide it. The surprise. The softness. That stunned little breath she takes, like she wasn’t prepared to see Rio like this.

The night drags on with Jen and Rio trailing closely behind Agatha.

Jen spends most of it leaning in to whisper names into Agatha’s ear just in time for each handshake, saving their boss from blank stares and awkward greetings. 

Here and there, Rio helps out too, usually when Jen’s caught up somewhere else. But every time she gets close enough to whisper, she catches that now-familiar scent of lavender clinging to Agatha’s skin, and it takes everything in Rio not to stupidly lose her composure right there.

The benefit wraps up right at midnight, and to Rio’s surprise, it ends with Agatha leaning in close, her breath warm against her ear as she whispers words Rio didn’t expect.

“If you were trying to make an impression, then consider me...thoroughly impressed.”

And it requires every bit of strength Rio has not to think about it for the rest of the night.

Chapter 2

Chapter Summary

“I’m serious. A fort will keep the thunder out. My dad swore by it. And your blanket would make a solid roof, by the way.”

“No, Rio. I’m thirty-eight. I’m not building a fort with you.”

“You sure? It’ll be so much fun.”

“Build your own damn fort. I’m going to try and get some sleep.” Her boss sounds annoyed, but Rio can still hear the endearing smile tucked behind the words.

“Do you want me to hold your hand? My mom said that helps too.”

“Now you’re just being annoying,” Agatha says, tossing a pillow at her assistant, who can’t stop grinning to herself in the dark.

Chapter Notes

it seems i’ve thoroughly enjoyed writing this, thanks to all the love it’s been receiving during this very special month. so much so that what was supposed to be a neat two-chapter story has now been hijacked by the ‘muses’ and turned into three. thank you for reading, cheering, and letting this story live a little longer.

The day after the benefit, and Rio finds herself an absolute mess. 

She’s been stuck in fashion hell for the last fifteen minutes, trying on half the stuff Billy and Alice gleefully stuffed into her closet over the weekend, and none of it makes any sense. She can’t tell which shirt goes with which pants, or why there are suddenly so many damn buttons, or why every top makes her look like she’s about to lead a prayer circle. And she doesn’t even go to church.

To make things worse, her so-called fairy godmothers are both missing in action.

Billy’s off at a friend’s house doing God knows what, and Alice hasn’t even come home. She’d texted last night saying she’d found “the cutest redhead at this gig” and had zero plans of returning before lunch.

Which leaves Rio completely alone and wildly unqualified to decide what to wear to work today.

She groans as she holds up yet another pair of pants that make her look like a substitute art teacher on sabbatical. Maybe she should just throw on jeans, her usual band tee, and slap a blazer over it, same as any other day. But then she imagines the look Agatha would give her, and tosses the pants into the growing mountain on her bed.

Rio, without question, is a lost cause when it comes to fashion. But Agatha had actually complimented her last night — “thoroughly impressed,” she said. Thoroughly. And for some completely stupid, godforsaken reason, Rio is now on a mission to not completely ruin the streak.

She throws on another outfit. This time, a pencil skirt, a tucked-in button-down, and a pair of modest heels. Suddenly, she looks a lot like Jen and all the other perfectly polished women on their floor who somehow never spill coffee on themselves or forget their lunch in the fridge. It’s a little terrifying.

Still, she thinks Agatha might actually like this. Or at least not roll her eyes at it.

“This’ll do, I guess,” she mumbles to herself before turning to check her own ass in the mirror. 

Anyone would want to look at this ass, she thinks, then gets a little freaked out that she’s basically wearing this just so people will start staring at her butt. 

Well, she does want people to look. If ‘people’ pertains to a 38-year-old editor-in-chief of a top fashion magazine, living on the Upper East Side of New York.

But then the real horror sets in. Not the heels. Not even the skirt, which, to be honest, should have been the scariest part, considering Rio never liked wearing them in the first place. And not even the weird, corporate Barbie metamorphosis staring back at her in the mirror. 

No. 

It’s the deeply uncomfortable, unsettling truth that Agatha Harkness’s opinion suddenly matters to her. Way more than it should.

The unsettling truth then gets smacked into full confirmation the second Agatha walks past her desk, slows just enough to give her a look, says absolutely nothing, and disappears into her office. 

Rio stares at the door, suddenly questioning her entire outfit choice. Did Agatha hate it? Because if that look meant what she thinks it did, then she may have just squeezed herself into a skirt, wobbled around in heels, and sacrificed her feet to the gods of Blistertown for absolutely nothing.

“Great. All this pain and not even a single hello,” Rio grumbles, earning a confused, scrutinizing look from Jen across the room, completely clueless about her coworker’s inner turmoil.

“What’s going on?” Agatha asks as Rio sets the coffee on her desk hours later. “What is this?”

“Your extra-hot triple-shot oat milk flat white?”

Agatha doesn’t even look at the cup. Her eyes are already sweeping over Rio from head to toe.

“Not my coffee. You. This. Whatever this is,” she replies, gesturing vaguely at Rio as if she’s been itching to ask all morning. “Did you finally retire the walking billboard look for every obscure New York punk band you’re obsessed with?”

Rio looks down at her mid-thigh skirt, suddenly feeling about twelve.

“I thought you said I should start looking like I take the job seriously.”

Agatha regards her, then brings the cup to her lips, eyes locked on Rio the whole time.

“So, am I supposed to assume this whole ensemble is for my benefit?”

Rio’s throat goes dry, and her face suddenly feels way too warm. 

“What? No, no, it’s not like that! I just didn’t want to give you another reason to fire me or anything. This has nothing to do with you.” She wants to disappear, anywhere but here, at how much she’s stumbling over her words. Lately, she’s been turning into a headless chicken every time this woman is around.

“I see.” Agatha gives her another long look. “Well, if you’re going to keep playing dress-up, at least make sure you don’t look like you looted someone else’s closet. It’s a bit…distracting.” She lets the words settle, then adds, almost offhand, “You’re actually more tolerable when you just look like yourself.” A beat. Then a flick of her hand, her signature move when dismissing her employees. “Off you go.”

Rio nods slowly, cheeks noticeably warmer as she tries to shove down the ridiculous thought that maybe, just maybe, Agatha actually likes her the way she is. Or maybe that’s just her delusions talking.

She reaches the door, then pauses and turns back. “I'm not actually obsessed with them.” 

“What?” 

“Most of the bands on my shirts. You assumed I’m obsessed with them. Well, I’m not. I don’t actually listen to them that much. I just really like the shirts. They look cool. The graphics, mostly. I’m actually more into boygenius, Snail Mail, Fourth Wanderers…you know, indie rock, kind of folky stuff.”

Agatha leans back in her chair, eyeing Rio like she’s not quite sure what to do with that information.

“I have no idea who any of those are,” she says, and Rio can’t tell if she’s being serious.

“Seriously? Not even boygenius? Phoebe Bridgers? Lucy Dacus? Julien Baker?” Rio blinks. “They’re, like, everywhere now. You’re kidding, right?”

“Do I look like I’m kidding?” Agatha replies bluntly. When Rio doesn’t answer, she calmly looks back down at the proofs on her desk. “Now, if you’re done sharing your niche music preferences, I suggest you get back to work. Some of us have an actual magazine to run.”

Rio clears her throat, the change in Agatha's tone biting harder than she anticipated. It’s as if last night never happened. As if she hadn’t whispered in Rio’s ear or looked at her the way she did. 

“Right. Of course. Just let me know if you need anything. I’ll be at my desk.”

Agatha doesn’t answer, doesn’t even look up. So Rio walks out, trying (and failing) not to let it get under her skin. That ended up being the only conversation they had all day. Everything else had come through email, with Jen acting as the go-between for every instruction.

Days blur into weeks, and everything just falls back into the same old routine. 

Coffee runs, dry cleaning, delivering the book, picking up proofs, sitting through meetings, jotting down notes. No one asks for her opinion anymore. No surprise invitations to Agatha’s office to look through articles like before. No more offhand remarks or lingering stares. It’s as if a switch has flipped yet again, and Agatha has rebuilt the wall between them. Like it was never down to begin with.

“She’s back,” Rio announces as Alice and Billy walk into the apartment, arms full of paper bags rustling with chips, canned beer, and a couple of sodas.

It’s The One Where We Drink Night: a sacred tradition where she and Alice park themselves on the couch, quote their favorite Friends episodes, and drink until probably past midnight.

Tonight, though, Billy’s crashing the party. His mom’s working late, which apparently earns him chips and soda — because Alice still refuses to let him near beer, but if it were up to Rio, she’d hand him a can — and front-row seats to two lesbians probably arguing, once again, over whether Ross ever actually deserved Rachel. Or whether it really should’ve been Rachel and Monica. Or maybe that one ex-girlfriend of Chandler’s who looked exactly like Julia Roberts.

“Who’s back?” Billy calls out, popping his head into Rio’s room just in time to catch her lying on her small, slightly lumpy bed, feet resting against the wall, and head hanging upside down like a bat.

“Satan’s mistress,” Rio mumbles, sitting up and tugging on her favorite I Have a Nut Allergy hoodie, the one that’s definitely not about anything you’d find in a snack aisle. 

She hops off the bed and trudges into the living room to join them.

“Didn’t you say she was starting to warm up to you? What happened to that?” Alice calls from the kitchen, sliding cans of beer into the fridge.

“I don’t know,” Rio admits, rubbing the back of her neck. “Out of nowhere, she just turns back into this cold, distant, indifferent succubus bitch.” She runs a hand through her hair, suddenly regretting the word. It felt almost unapologetic before, but now it leaves a sour taste in her mouth. “It’s like I’m walking on eggshells all the time, never knowing which Agatha I’m gonna get.”

“Maybe she just needs more wooing,” Billy offers, plopping onto the couch. “Mom says women like to be wooed. Pretty sure that applies to your hot boss too. You have been wooing her, right?”

Rio squints at him. “What are you talking about?”

“What have you been doing to make her warm up to you?”

Rio thinks for a second. Has she even done anything? As far as she can remember, Agatha simply began warming up to her, in glimpses, like flashes of sunlight through clouds. 

She hadn’t really done much or tried. She was just being herself. “Nothing,” she replies.

“Well, there’s your problem.”

Rio throws her hands up. “And how exactly am I supposed to woo her?” She even throws in the air quotes. “She barely talks to me anymore. It’s like I don’t exist again. And every time I see her, I just want to strangle her and pin her to the wall and —” kiss her is what her brain offers, unhelpfully.

“— slit her throat,” she finishes instead.

Alice, now joining Billy on the couch, lets out a short laugh. “Well, aren’t you a romantic?” 

Rio just rolls her eyes and nudges her over to make space. 

“What Billy’s trying to say,” Alice goes on, curling her legs up under her, “is maybe try winning her over the old-fashioned way. Bring her flowers. Cook her something. Bake cookies or whatever.” She gives Rio a teasing look. “Didn’t you used to do all that cheesy stuff to woo a couple of girls back in college?”

Rio snorts as she grabs the remote. 

“Absolutely not. I’m not wooing my boss like she’s some kind of girlfriend.”

Alice side-eyes her. “Then why are you this upset about her ignoring you?”

Rio pauses, reaching for an excuse. “Because…I thought I was finally making progress. You know, career-wise. Like, maybe getting a seat at the table. Even if it’s the kiddie table, with plastic chairs and juice boxes.” She sighs. “I thought I was this close to actually being allowed to write something.” 

Then she hits play on the first episode.

“You know denial isn’t good for you. It’s not very healthy,” Billy says, chips crunching between words. “Mom says it’s like trying to mop up a flood with paper towels. Pointless, messy, and you just end up on your ass. She also says it’s like holding in a fart at a dinner party. The longer you pretend it’s not there, the more it hurts, and eventually everyone’s gonna notice anyway. Might as well just let it out before you explode or ruin the couch.”

Rio shoots him a look, part annoyed, part grossed out. “Did you really have to use a fart metaphor?” she cringes, shaking her head. She can already imagine how painful it would be to be stuck in a room with Billy and his mom. Endless advice, weird sayings, and unsolicited therapy. It’d be torture. And yet, the kid kind of has a point. But is she really in denial? And if she is, what exactly is she denying?

After hours of zoning out and only half listening to Billy and Alice argue over the best Friends character, Rio now lies on her bed, staring up at the ceiling, wondering how the hell she managed to land herself in this predicament. Wanting Agatha’s attention, wanting her approval, and, God help her, just wanting to be around her, in whatever manner she could justify.

And finally, she traces it all back to that one day at Pride. If she hadn’t lied and called in sick, maybe none of this would have happened. That was the moment. The spark that lit the fuse.

Because ever since then, it’s like someone has turned the volume up on Agatha. 

Every little detail is suddenly louder and impossible to ignore. 

The way she rakes her fingers through her hair when she’s exhausted. The little shoulder shimmy she does when something actually gets to her, even though her face stays all business. The way her mouth tightens, lips pressed in that exact shape Rio now recognizes as seconds from homicide.

At first, Rio tells herself it’s just a crush. Harmless. Fleeting. Fucking temporary. 

Her boss is gorgeous and hot and somehow walks around radiating the gravitational pull of a dying star. And as Alice once pointed out, Rio is not blind. It doesn’t have to mean anything. Just a little brain static. A hormonal hiccup. A blip. A glitch in the gay matrix. Nothing that actually matters.

But then she slowly becomes aware of Agatha, and painfully aware of just how aware she is. Agatha lives in her head now, occupying space like she’s built a summer home inside. 

Rio prints files she could just email, just so she has an excuse to walk into Agatha’s office and stare into those clear blue eyes. She even gets a little thrill when she hears Agatha’s voice on the phone when she’s passing calls. Sometimes, she also walks over to Jen’s desk for no real reason, just to catch a quick glimpse of Agatha through the office glass.

Rio does this. Frequently. Almost obsessively.

And now, unfortunately, Rio comes to a very real, very inconvenient conclusion: 

She’s into her boss.

She’s into Agatha.

Rio is in an unreasonably cheerful mood as she breezes back into their office, having just completed a covert operation earlier that morning. Fortunately, she’s made a few friends in the bullpen who haven’t asked too many questions about why she showed up so early, only to loiter downstairs for almost an hour.

That had been the plan: to wait until Jen and, more importantly, their boss are already in. That way, no one will make the connection between Rio and the peace lily sitting on Agatha’s desk.

It’s a solid plan. One of her finest. And to be fair, Agatha’s office could use a little green. Everything in there is white, black, gray, and just the faintest hint of purple, if you squint.

She walks to her desk and, out of habit, steals a quick glimpse toward Agatha’s office. 

But one look at her boss and she nearly stumbles.

Agatha is glued to her laptop, fingers furiously speeding over the keys. Her jaw is taut, her brow furrowed low, and she looks either aggravated or just terrifyingly focused. 

Then Rio angles her head, scanning for the peace lily she’d dropped off earlier. But it’s gone. Not on the desk. Not by the window. Not anywhere. 

Her heart does a small, awkward flop. 

Either Agatha moved it somewhere out of sight, or it’s currently living in a trash bin.

Rio doesn’t know what kind of plant Agatha might actually like, but a peace lily feels like a safe bet. They’re supposed to turn bad energy into good energy, and help make things a little more peaceful between people. And that’s all Rio really wants: a little peace between her and Agatha.

Rio’s fingers are mid-sentence on her keyboard when a sharp clack slices through the office, followed by Agatha’s venomous voice: “I said review the layout, not tear it to shreds like an amateur with a goddamn glue stick. Do you even realize how much this mistake is going to cost us? That’s hundreds of thousands of dollars down the drain in ad space and printing, because you couldn’t be bothered to double-check before sending it to print. This is not some student paper stapled together in a dorm. Don’t even bother finishing the day. Pack your things. You’re fired!”

Rio looks up just in time to see her boss slam the phone into its receiver, eyes locked on the handset like it might have the nerve to ring again. 

Curious, and maybe a little nervous, Rio grabs a few random files and makes her way across the room, passing Jen’s desk. “Okay, what fresh hell is that all about?” 

Jen doesn’t even look up. “Someone left her a psycho plant.”

Rio feels her stomach sink like stone. 

“I’m sorry, what?” Her voice jumps an octave. “What do you mean, psycho plant?”

“A peace lily, of all things. Can you imagine? Those things are basically the houseplants of death. You only send them when someone has died. They’re funeral flowers. Obviously, Agatha had it chucked immediately. I suggested calling security, but she was so pissed I think she’d rather track the person down herself. People these days are just so messed up.” 

Rio feels her throat closing from the inside. The internet had said nothing about peace lilies being floral condolences. She had thought they were very pretty, calming even. Exactly the kind of thing someone like Agatha, with her ice-cold office, could use. How did she mess up the one thing she’s usually good at?

Her eyes return to her boss, where fury is practically carved into every line of her face. She looks like she could incinerate someone with a single, precision-grade glare. Rio forces herself to swallow.

Oh boy. She didn’t just annoy her.

She poked the bear.

And now the bear looks more than ready to maul.

“You didn’t happen to see who dropped it on her desk, did you?” Jen asks, snapping Rio’s attention back to her. “I think she’s really on a warpath, trying to hunt down whoever had the deranged idea to do it.”

“Nope. I just got here, remember?”

Rio’s not exactly a great liar, but when her job (or more importantly, her life) is on the line, she can fake it well enough. Still, her stomach twists into a tight, guilty knot. 

So much for the peaceful gesture. This is not how peace is meant to start. 

Later that night, Rio gets sent to a penthouse belonging to someone who goes by just Kingo, an actor-turned-designer who apparently ditched his last name the moment fashion decided he was a genius. 

Jen had told her she only needed to “pick something up for Agatha,” which sounded simple enough. What she conveniently forgot to mention was that Kingo lives somewhere in the clouds and hosts parties on random weeknights. 

The second Rio steps through the massive double doors, she’s swallowed by music, chatter, and the very specific scent of wealth. The place is ridiculous: sleek marble floors, gold accents on anything that doesn’t move, and movie posters everywhere featuring Kingo himself in varying degrees of heroic brooding. 

The whole penthouse feels less like a home and more like a shrine. To himself.

“Welcome! May I help you?” comes a warm, slightly accented voice.

Rio turns to find a man with a kind face and a gentle belly. 

“Hi. Yes. I’m looking for Kingo?” she says, half-shouting over the music.

The man places a hand over his chest and gives a small, respectful bow. 

“My name is Karun. My master is just this way.”

Before Rio can even process the whole “my master” thing, he’s already gliding through the crowd, with her trailing behind him. They weave through clusters of people until they reach Kingo himself, sipping something bright pink and animatedly telling a group about the time he landed a movie with James Cameron while tagging along to Cannes with Harry Styles.

Kingo doesn’t notice them at first, too busy acting out a slow-motion walk down a red carpet, one hand on his chest, the other pretending to block out fake camera flashes.

Karun clears his throat politely. “Excuse me, sir. You have a guest.” Kingo spins mid-pose, eyes landing on Rio like he’s assessing a new accessory. Then his face lights up with recognition, or something close to it. “This is Miss...?” Karun adds, gesturing toward Rio to fill in the blank.

“Uh, hi. Yes. I’m Rio. Agatha Harkness sent me. I’m here to pick something up for her.”

“Ah! You must be the new Jen,” Kingo says, striding toward her with his arms thrown wide. 

Rio lets out a small laugh, unsure if she’s supposed to hug him or dodge. 

“They didn’t tell me Agatha’s new assistant was such a beauty,” he adds with a wink, already turning away before she can respond. Then he snaps his fingers, more out of habit than rudeness. “Karun, would you mind grabbing the folder from my nightstand? The black one with the gold clasp. Chop-chop. Let’s not keep this lovely guest waiting.”

It isn’t long before Karun returns, holding a black folder. “My latest design,” Kingo says proudly, taking it and handing it to Rio. “It’s a sketch of what Agatha will be wearing in Berlin. Part of my next collection. No one’s seen it yet. Not even the press. So, you know, don’t go losing it.” He gives her another quick wink. 

“Of course. Got it,” Rio says.

“And now,” he announces, looping an arm around her shoulders, “since you’re working for the great Agatha Harkness, I’m guessing you could use a very strong drink.”

Rio opens her mouth to protest, but Kingo's already dragging her to the bar. “The strongest thing you’ve got for this lovely lady,” he tells the bartender, “who, in my humble opinion, has the hardest job in the world.” He takes the drink the bartender hands over and offers it to her with a flourish.

Rio snorts despite herself and accepts the drink. “Thanks.”

“Now that you’ve got a drink in hand, I should go mingle. Clearly, I’m very popular tonight.” He gives her a quick, almost fond salute before slipping back into the crowd, already laughing at something someone shouts his way. “Lovely meeting you!” he calls over his shoulder.

“You too,” Rio calls back, watching him disappear. She looks down at the drink in her hand and is about to take a sip when a voice beside her chimes in.

“Last time I drank something like that at one of Kingo’s parties, I ended up in the fetal position in someone’s bathtub.”

Rio jumps a little, then turns to see a tall woman leaning against the bar. “Oh,” she says, immediately setting the glass back down. “Yeah…probably not a great idea.”

The woman chuckles. “Smart choice.” Then she holds out a hand. “Hela Odinson.”

Rio’s eyes widen. “Hela Odinson? As in The Wall Street Journal Hela Odinson?”

“No, that’s my twin.”

“Hela has a twin?”

The woman lets out a full laugh this time. 

“Sorry, I’m just messing with you. But I do wish I had a twin. Instead, I got stuck with two idiot brothers.” Her tone is dry, but there’s a spark of humor in her eyes. “Though I see you’ve read my work.”

“I’ve read almost everything you’ve written. I even used your essay collection for my college paper.”

“Look at you, flattering a journalist’s oversized ego. Did you also mention my devastating good looks and irresistible charm?” She watches Rio with a smile that feels just a little too warm. 

The latter offers a nervous smile, unsure what to say. But Hela leans in, closing the space between them just a little. “So, what is it you do? A writer too, I’m guessing?”

“Um, yes. I do write, but—” 

“Well then, you should send me something,” Hela says. “I’d love to read it.”

“Wait, are you serious?” Rio blinks, and Hela just nods. “Wow, that’d be really great,” she says, then pauses as the reality catches up to her. “But the thing is, I haven’t written anything new in a while. I’ve kind of been tied up lately…working as Agatha Harkness’s assistant.”

Hela stops mid-sip. “Oh, you’re kidding, right? Really? Agatha Harkness?” she says, as if simply saying the name puts a foul taste in her mouth.

“Yes. She’s my boss. That’s why I’m here,” Rio replies, holding up the black folder.

Hela lets out a low whistle and takes another sip of her drink. “That’s rough. You’re not gonna last.”

“Excuse me?”

“I’m just saying, any job tied to Agatha Harkness doesn’t exactly come with long-term potential,” Hela says with a dry smile. “The woman’s ruthless. A total sadist, and not the fun kind, either.”

Rio isn’t sure why she feels the need to say anything, but the words come out before she can stop them. “Okay, sure, she’s tough. But if Agatha were a man, no one would be calling her anything. They’d just be talking about how damn good she is at what she does.”

Hela gives her a look. “Ah. We’ve got ourselves a worshipper.”

“I don’t worship her.” She is, however, teetering dangerously close to it. 

“Well,” Hela says, laughing under her breath, “you seem very smart. And way too nice to be working for someone like Agatha. I’m just saying, someone with a soul doesn’t deserve to be taking orders from someone who doesn’t. That job doesn’t really suit you, darling.” She gives Rio a once-over, the smirk never quite leaving her face.

Rio takes a deep breath and just gives her a tight smile. “I think I’ll head out.”

Hela chuckles, clearly sensing she might’ve touched a nerve. “Alright,” she says, reaching into her clutch and pulling out a card. “No hard feelings. And I really meant what I said about your writing. Send it my way anytime, or whenever you finally escape Agatha’s clutches.” She hands it over with a small, crooked smile.

Rio takes the card politely. “Thanks. And…thanks again for the heads-up,” she says, nodding toward the abandoned drink before turning and walking off.

The next morning, Rio shows up early at work, just like last time. She walks into Agatha’s office before anyone else arrives, carrying a small bunny ear cactus, and carefully sets it down on the desk. 

Clipped underneath is a printed note explaining the real meaning of a peace lily, along with a short handwritten message that reads: “In case things got lost in translation.”

She’d spent hours last night scrolling through the internet, making sure the message couldn’t possibly be misunderstood again. Feeling cautiously hopeful, Rio turns to head to the bullpen, only to freeze when she sees Agatha standing in the doorway. 

What the hell? Why is she here this early?

“What exactly do you think you’re doing?” Agatha asks. She’s in a trench coat, hair pulled back into a ponytail, and Rio tries not to get too distracted by how unfairly good she looks, given the situation. Agatha’s eyes land on the cactus on her desk. “What is this? Are you trying to be funny?” 

“No! No, of course not.” Rio nearly trips over herself as she quickly grabs the note and holds it out to her. “I didn’t mean for the peace lily to come off like that. That wasn’t the point. Just…here. Read this.”

Agatha reads the note silently, then looks back up at her. “This still doesn’t explain why you keep leaving little green footprints all over my office. Do I look like someone who wants to adopt your plants?” 

“I’m just trying to make peace,” Rio confesses. Agatha only stares at her, so she keeps going. “I don’t know what I did, or why you started ignoring me again, but whatever it is, I’m trying to make up for it.”

Agatha’s brow arches. “All this because you think I’m ignoring you?”

“Yes, and we both know it.”

Agatha gives her a long look. “So what, you think you’ve got me all figured out now?”

“No. Not even close. But I’d really like to.”

“Excuse me?” 

“I said I want to get to know you,” Rio says. “And I think you're a little curious about me too.”

Agatha stares at her for a moment, then lets out a small, dry laugh. “Well, aren’t you bold, saying things that blunt to your boss in a job you haven’t even had long enough to settle into.”

Rio glances at herself, almost questioning it. 

Four months, isn’t that long enough? It sure feels long, given everything that’s happened. 

“My dad used to say that too, that I get too blunt and honest when I’m frustrated. I always told him I got it from him, and he’d just laugh, because it was true.”

She looks at Agatha, who still seems to be processing what she just said.

“But if I read you wrong, if I thought you wanted to know me the way I wanted to know you, then I apologize,” Rio adds. “Just know the peace lily wasn’t meant to mock you.” She reaches for the cactus, ready to take it back, when Agatha finally speaks.

“I have to go to Carlton later this afternoon. Make sure you finish all your work before lunch so you can come with me,” she says. They look at each other for a beat. “And leave the plant,” she adds. “But that’ll still be your responsibility. I don’t know how to take care of it, so don’t expect anything from me. If it dies from neglect, I’m tossing it straight in the bin.”

“Of course,” Rio says, trying not to sound too pleased.

Agatha walks past her to her desk. “And would you please wipe that grin off your face,” she tosses over her shoulder. “No one should be that cheerful at seven in the damn morning.”

But Rio can’t help it, her smile only grows as she slips out of Agatha’s office.

The thing, whatever it is, just keeps going. One outing turns into two, then three, and before Rio knows it, three weeks have passed. Agatha brings her everywhere now. 

Meetings. Site visits. Run-throughs. Dress fittings. Fabric picking. Private showroom appointments in tucked-away studios. Walkthroughs for campaign photoshoots. One painfully silent lunch with a notoriously terrifying designer who looked at Rio like she was chewing with her mouth open. And then there was that one morning Agatha made her choose between two dresses that looked exactly the same, except one was “winter white” and the other was “bone.”

“Let Jen know I’m meeting Pepper tomorrow,” Agatha says from the backseat, eyes still on her phone as they speed down the highway to the Hamptons. “Tell her to schedule it over lunch. I want a believable excuse to escape that woman whenever I feel like it.” She sighs, setting her phone down. “I still don’t understand why that old man insists on getting married out here. There are plenty of overpriced venues in the city that could match whatever ridiculous spectacle he’s planning for wife number seven.”

“Seven? Seriously?” Rio asks from her corner of the backseat, keeping a safe distance between them.

“Oh, you’d be surprised how often men feel the urge to stick their dicks into something new the second they get bored,” Agatha says, sliding on her sunglasses. “I just wonder how many wives he’ll burn through before his sad wrinkled dick finally gives out and dies.”

Rio looks over at Agatha, then turns to the window. The sky is clear, but the air feels heavy, like rain is just around the corner. Nature’s got jokes today. All that money, and not one of these rich people thought to check the weather for their big wedding. Or maybe it’s just God’s way of messing with the wealthy.

“If you hate coming out here so much, then why even bother?”

Agatha finally turns to her, sunglasses on, but Rio can still see her eyes through the tint. 

“Because, unfortunately, that dreaded dick owns a sizable share of Tony’s company. And if things go sideways with these ego-driven idiots, Ross might just yank it straight from Tony’s ass.” She leans back in her seat. “That’s why we’re here. Stuck dealing with the same bullshit, just a different side of it.”

They arrive at the venue for what’s supposed to be a dreamy beach wedding. 

The ceremony goes off without a hitch, aside from the strong wind, the sky turning the color of bruised peaches, and the nearly torrential downpour shortly before the reception. 

“Perhaps this is what happens when a 78-year-old man marries someone young enough to be his granddaughter fresh out of high school,” Agatha had said when she found Rio in the corner and told her it was time to head home. 

“Okay, so aside from your lunch with Ms. Potts tomorrow, you have a video conference scheduled with Nocturne France and Madrid first thing in the morning,” Rio reads from her phone as rain lashes against the windows. “Then there’s a brief walkthrough with the stylists scheduled at three.”

Rain drums harder against the windows, and every so often, thunder rumbles in the distance. It’s not loud, but close enough that Rio catches the subtle flinch her boss makes. She is angled slightly toward the window and hasn’t said a word since the car pulled out.

“Jen also said some people from Harper’s dropped by your office,” Rio continues. “Didn’t leave much detail. Just said they needed to speak with you in person. Want me to send a reply?”

Agatha doesn’t respond.

“Agatha?”

“What?” Agatha blinks, as if she’s just been pulled from a deep thought. “Oh, right. Just tell them I’ll be available the day after tomorrow. Afternoon only. After all my actual priorities.” 

Rio lowers her phone, watching her a beat longer. “You alright?”

Agatha turns her head slightly. “What makes you think I’m not?”

“Sorry. You just seem a little—” 

She doesn’t get to finish the thought, because the car suddenly slows to a stop. 

Rio leans forward, spotting the problem right away. There’s a large tree sprawled across the road, with a few people in ponchos gathered around it like they’ve been there for hours.

“What’s going on?” Agatha asks, sitting up straighter. “Why are we stopping?”

Javier looks at them through the rearview mirror. “There’s a big tree down up ahead, Miss Harkness. Looks like the wind took it out. It’s blocking the whole road.”

“Is there another way around it?” 

“I’ll check.” Javier opens the door, grabs an umbrella from the side pocket, and steps out into the rain.

With just the two of them left in the car, Rio casts another glance at Agatha, who appears even more tense now that they’re not moving. After a few minutes, Javier returns, brushing rain from his shoulders.

“Sorry, ma’am. Locals say the tree’s not getting cleared until sometime tomorrow. It’s too big to move without heavy equipment. The only option is to turn around.”

“Turn around?” Agatha echoes. “What do you mean turn around?” 

“I’m really sorry, Miss Harkness but we’ll have to find somewhere nearby to stay the night.”

She exhales slowly through her nose. “You’ve got to be fucking kidding me.”

The fifth hotel they pull into is more rundown than anything Agatha would normally set foot in. But stuck in Manorville with the miserable, godforsaken timing of a storm and every other place fully booked, it’s their only shot at staying dry and maybe getting a half-decent night’s sleep.

“One?” Rio asks, standing there soaked through, arms wrapped tightly around herself. “Just one room?”

“Yes, ma’am. All the other rooms were booked earlier after the road closed.” 

Rio leans forward, dripping water onto the counter. 

“Are you absolutely sure? Could you maybe check again? Sometimes rooms don’t show up in the system right away, right? Or maybe there’s one that just hasn’t been cleaned yet? That happens.”

“I’m really sorry but I already checked twice. Just the one.”

Rio can feel her teeth wanting to chatter from the cold, and maybe from stress, too. The receptionist keeps typing like this happens every day and isn’t, in fact, a minor crisis for Rio personally.

“Well?” Agatha says as she walks up, sliding her phone into her coat pocket, fresh off a call and clearly out of patience. “How long does it take to book a damn room in this town?”

“They’ve got a room,” Rio says. “Just one.”

Agatha raises an eyebrow. “And?”

“And it’s yours. I’ll see if there’s another place nearby. Javier might’ve found something already.”

“He did,” Agatha replies. “Said something about a motel ten minutes down the road.”

“Great,” Rio nods. “I’ll have him swing back and pick me up.”

“That won’t be necessary. I already told him to pick us up here tomorrow morning.”

Rio blinks. “Us?” 

Agatha raises an eyebrow. “You didn’t seriously think I was going to sleep here alone, did you?” She gestures vaguely at the sad little lobby: the flickering light, the lumpy floral couch that’s probably older than both of them, and the faint smell of damp carpet. “What if I need something later? Are you planning to drive back through a storm? Or do you have teleportation powers I should know about?”

Rio’s head is still reeling from the fact that she and Agatha will be sharing a room, she can’t even get a word out.

“And if you think I’m sleeping in some roadside motel, you’ve clearly lost your mind,” her boss adds, turning to the receptionist. “We’ll take the room. And if you’ve got an extra mattress, kindly send it up.”

Rio blinks again. “You’re actually serious?”

“What? You’d rather your boss sleep on the floor?”

Rio opens her mouth to argue, then closes it again. 

Because of course Agatha Harkness would say that with a straight face.

And of course Rio’s dumb enough to follow her up the stairs anyway.

Rio steps out of the shower, wrapped in a robe the hotel provided. She’d usually just wear a tank top and boxers to sleep, she never minded people seeing her like that. But something about Agatha makes her hesitate. Confidence doesn’t come as easy around her boss.

She finds Agatha sitting on the bed, back against the headboard, flipping through channels like she’s annoyed at all of them. The volume’s cranked up so loud, Rio can barely hear the rain or the distant rumble of thunder outside.

Rio hovers by the bathroom door for a second, then steps further into the room, drying her hair with the towel draped over her shoulders. She squints at the blaring screen. 

“Does it have to be this loud?” she calls over the noise.

But Agatha doesn’t answer. She just keeps flipping through the channels: news, weather, an old black-and-white movie, static, then news again. Her eyes don’t move much. Nothing seems to catch her attention, and it’s pretty clear she’s not finding whatever it is she’s looking for.

Rio tries again. “You’re not even watching anything.”

Agatha keeps flipping. “Everything’s either depressing or stupid.” Then she sighs, tosses the remote aside, and leans her head back against the headboard.

A second later, thunder crashes. Loud and close, like it struck right outside the window.

Rio jumps a little. “Jesus,” her breath catches, placing a hand on her chest.

The lights cut out all at once. The TV goes black. The room immediately descends into darkness, punctuated only by the sound of rain pounding hard against the windows. 

“Shit. The power’s out,” she adds, stating the obvious as she squints toward where Agatha had been sitting. For a second, Rio thinks her boss might say something. But when her eyes adjust to the dark, she realizes Agatha hasn’t even moved.

She’s sitting stiffly against the headboard, knees now pulled up, hands over her ears, and eyes shut tight. Rio watches from where she’s standing, and just like she guessed earlier, Agatha’s afraid of thunder.

“Agatha?” She takes a step forward, careful not to trip on anything. “Hey, it’s okay. It’s just thunder.” She kneels gently at the edge of the bed. “You’re alright. It’s just the storm. The lights will come back on soon.”

But before Rio can even reach out to comfort her, there’s a sudden knock at the door. 

She gets up quickly and crosses the room in a few careful steps. 

When she opens the door, a hotel staff member is standing there with a flashlight, rain dripping from his coat. “Sorry for the trouble, ma’am,” he says, slightly out of breath. “Lightning hit a nearby transformer. Power’s gonna be out for a while.” Then he hands her a small emergency lantern. “This should help for now. Just press the button on the side.”

Rio nods. “Alright. Thanks for coming up and letting us know.”

She closes the door and switches on the lantern, filling the room with a soft, warm glow, and sees Agatha still on the bed. She's no longer covering her ears, and her eyes are open, but her face remains blank and seemingly distant. Rio walks over and sets the lantern down on the nightstand. “Hey, you okay?” she asks her again, even though it’s clear she’s not. But what is Rio even supposed to say?

Then, as if the light turned on a switch, Agatha sits up straighter and takes a long, deep breath before schooling her face into something calmer and more neutral. “I’m fine,” she adds, grabbing for her phone, only to scowl. “Of course, it’s dead. Damn it.” Without hesitation, she flings it against the nearest wall, causing the phone to crack before bouncing to the floor. She then turns to Rio like nothing happened. “Hand over your phone. I need to check a few emails.”

Rio stares at her with wide eyes. “Should I be worried you’ll throw mine too if it’s dead?” Agatha only shoots her a glare. “Because it is,” Rio adds, holding up her hands. “I forgot to charge it.”

Her boss rolls her eyes. “What kind of assistant forgets to charge her phone?”

“The kind who ends up unexpectedly stuck in a storm with her boss,” Rio replies, deadpan.

Agatha lets out a tired sigh and leans back against the headboard. 

“Fine. Just make sure to tell Jen to have a new one waiting on my desk the minute we’re back in the city.”

Another rumble of thunder rolls through. It’s not as loud as the last one, but Agatha still flinches somewhat. She curls her hand into a fist, and she appears more annoyed than anything else, as if she is frustrated with herself for reacting at all.

Rio notices it but doesn’t say anything at first. Then, softly, “Let’s just try to get some sleep.” She walks over to the other side of the bed where the extra mattress has been set on the floor and lies down without another word. The rain keeps pouring outside, but neither of them says anything.

Another low rumble of thunder comes through, a bit louder this time, but not enough to make the windows shudder. From the floor, Rio hears the soft rustle of sheets and the creak of the mattress above her.

“You okay?”

There’s a pause. Then Agatha replies, “I’m fine.”

“Are you sure?” 

“I said I’m fine, Rio.” 

Rio doesn’t believe it, but she doesn’t push.

She stares at the ceiling for a while, trying to focus on the sound of rain tapping against the windows. But she can still hear Agatha shifting above her, like she just can’t get comfortable. 

“You know,” she attempts, trying to redirect Agatha’s attention away from the storm outside, “when I was a kid, every time it rained like this, my dad and I would build a fort in my room. Blankets, pillows, chairs, whatever we could find. He always said as long as we were inside, no thunder could get us.”

There’s no reply, but she keeps going. “I used to believe him. Even when I got older. I think I still do, a little.” Her eyes trace the faint lines on the ceiling.

“We also had this old flashlight shaped like a duck,” she continues. “Beak lit up yellow. It barely worked, but we always used that one for the fort. Dad said the warm light made it cozier. I think he just liked that it looked stupid.” She smiles to herself, eyes closing for a moment.

“He also said storms were just the sky throwing a tantrum. Like maybe it needed someone to talk to.”

Rio turns her head slightly toward the bed. “So if you hear me talking too much...just know I’m just giving the storm a conversation.” She pulls the blanket up to her chin. “He also used to say the best way to handle fear was to make something out of it. A story, a joke, a pillow fort.” She snorts.

There’s a pause. Long enough that she thinks maybe Agatha has already fallen asleep. But then:

“What does he do now? Still building pillow forts in his spare time?”

Rio’s smile fades a little and stares at the ceiling again. 

“No,” she says. “He died when I was fifteen. My mami followed four years later.”

“Oh,” Agatha says. She doesn’t follow it with I’m sorry , and Rio’s oddly thankful for it. Another beat, and then Agatha adds, “He sounds like he was a good dad. And I’m guessing your mom was, too.”

Rio nods, even if Agatha can’t see it. “Yeah. They were.”

The room falls silent again. The rain keeps its rhythm outside, but it starts sounding like it’s finally tired itself out. Rio figures that’s it. That her boss has finally drifted off, or at least lost interest in talking.

But then, softly, almost like she’s asking more out of instinct than intention. 

“What happened to them?”

Rio takes a breath, eyes still fixed on the ceiling. 

“My dad was a reporter. Always chasing something. Stories, leads, the truth, or whatever piece of it came closest. He was driving back from an out-of-town assignment, trying to make it in time for this writing competition I’d joined. He was exhausted, hadn’t slept in over a day.” She pauses, remembering how she was still clutching the medal she eventually won when she got the news. “He dozed off behind the wheel, and he just never made it.”

Agatha doesn’t respond but Rio can feel she’s still listening.

“And my mom. She always said her heart gave out when he died. I thought it was just a figure of speech, you know, something people say when they’re grieving. But it turned out she actually had heart disease. She never told anyone, not until it got really bad. She fought hard, though. For me. But Dad was the love of her life. So in the end, I don’t think anything could’ve kept her from finding her way back to him.”

Some people used to say they felt sorry for Rio. That it was a tragedy, losing both her parents so young. That she should be upset at them for leaving her behind. 

But Rio never was. 

She’d seen how much her parents loved each other. How deep that love ran. And if those same people had witnessed it, really seen it, they might’ve understood why one couldn’t live without the other. 

And her parents had loved Rio so deeply, it felt like it could last a lifetime. So she never felt unloved. If anything, she felt grateful. 

“And you’ve been on your own since?” Agatha asks.

“Not exactly. I was already legal when my mom died, so I didn’t go into foster care or anything like that. But our next-door neighbors, Lorna and her daughter Alice, who I grew up with, took me in. Lorna was like a second mom, and Alice felt more like a sister than a friend. So they were basically family.”

Another beat passes, and as if sensing where the conversation is headed, Agatha finally speaks.

“I’m guessing Alice was the one you were with at Pride? The girl with the red streak in her hair?”

“Yeah, that was her,” Rio says, a grin slowly spreading across her face. “She’s the reason I even went to that parade in the first place. And the baby gay we were reluctantly assigned to babysit.”

“The kid with the eyeliner?”

“The teenager with the eyeliner, yes.” Rio snorts. “Honestly, I’m surprised you even remember that.”

“Well, it wasn’t exactly subtle. It was the first time someone had the audacity to lie to my face.”

Rio lets out a real laugh. “Hey, in my defense, it was a harmless lie.”

“And in mine,” Agatha replies, “it was still deeply irritating.”

Rio laughs even harder, and to her astonishment, she hears a soft chuckle from Agatha too.

Another boom of thunder rolls through, and the laughing fades.

Rio wants to ask why it rattles Agatha so much, why every rumble seems to reach something deep. 

But there’s something in the way she reacts that tells Rio not to push. At least not tonight. So she keeps the question to herself.

Instead, as the thunder quiets down, she looks up toward the bed and says, “Do you want to build a fort?”

Agatha lets out a real laugh, with a small snort in the middle that makes Rio blink in surprise. It’s the first time she’s heard her boss laugh like that, and she can’t help but smile.

“I’m serious. A fort will keep the thunder out. My dad swore by it. And your blanket would make a solid roof, by the way.”

“No, Rio. I’m thirty-eight. I’m not building a fort with you.”

“You sure? It’ll be so much fun.”

“Build your own damn fort. I’m going to try and get some sleep.” Her boss sounds annoyed, but Rio can still hear the endearing smile tucked behind the words.

“Do you want me to hold your hand? My mom said that helps too.”

“Now you’re just being annoying,” Agatha says, tossing a pillow at her assistant, who can’t stop grinning to herself in the dark.

The days that follow feel like a fever dream for Rio. She still does her usual work, but it doesn’t feel as bad as it did when she first started. It doesn’t even feel like punishment anymore. 

In fact, being in the office actually starts to excite her, especially when she sneaks in extra potted plants for Agatha’s office early in the morning, before anyone else arrives. She leaves little notes under each one and sometimes catches a glimpse of Agatha trying not to smile or laugh at the things she wrote.

On a random Wednesday, she gives Agatha a flash drive full of songs she actually listens to. Her boss, of course, had acted like she wasn’t interested and couldn’t care less. But a few hours later, Rio catches her with earphones in, fingers drumming lightly on the desk. And when Rio teases her about it, Agatha doesn’t deny it, and just rolls her eyes. Which, to Rio, basically feels like a confession. She now considers it a personal achievement: being the one who finally got the great Agatha Harkness to listen to boygenius.

More than before, Rio keeps joining her boss in closed-door meetings and starts picking up on Agatha’s little tells. She always looks calm, in control, and like the most put-together person in the room. 

But Rio catches the small things: how she zones out for half a second, the sighs, the quick glances at her watch. How her face changes just a bit when someone talks too much. Those had become Rio’s cue to stage a fake phone call from the office, giving her and her boss the perfect excuse to leave.

Then there’s the magazine work again, where Rio somewhat helps Agatha sort through articles. 

At one point, Agatha even tells her she’s surprisingly good at deciding what goes in, at least when it’s political and not just runway gossip. That’s followed by Agatha casually asking where Rio plans to apply once she starts taking journalism seriously. It catches Rio off guard because she hadn’t even realized Agatha had noticed, let alone thought about it. But the latter just says, “Your heart’s in writing. I knew it the minute you walked into my office that first day.”

At the end of each day, Rio still delivers the book at Agatha’s townhouse, and sometimes she catches her in the home office or the living room, half-dressed and surrounded by a swarm of stylists. 

It doesn’t escape Rio that Agatha asks for her opinion every so often, even though she knows Rio has no clue about fashion, and even though it always earns a few confused, curious glances from the rest of the room. Still, Rio figures Agatha thinks she has an eye for what works, and maybe she’s right. 

Because, boy, Rio does have eyes. Especially when it comes to how a dress hugs Agatha’s waist just right, how a slit shows just enough leg, how black and navy make her skin practically glow. 

And, well, most of the dresses do an excellent job of pushing her breasts up in all the right ways. 

That’s usually the part where Rio has to remind herself to look literally anywhere else, and to stop looking like she might drool.

“Is there anything else?” Agatha asks, slipping on her coat as she looks at Rio. It's past seven on a Friday night, and the entire floor has emptied out, leaving only the two of them in Agatha's office as usual.

Rio double-checks her notes. “Nope. That’s everything. You’re free for the rest of the evening.”

“And you? Also free the rest of the evening?”

Rio blinks. “Me?”

“Well, since I’m miraculously free, I assume you are too. Or do you have some tragically average date waiting at a rooftop bar like the rest of the lost souls on this godforsaken floor?”

Rio chuckles. “No. No dates. No plans either. Alice is out of town for a gig.”

“Perfect. That means you’re coming with me tonight.”

“Coming where, exactly?”

“I’m in the mood for sea urchin pasta, truffle risotto, and maybe a whole Breton lobster flown in from Brittany. Oh, and a bottle of something older than both of us. You’ll join me while I indulge.” Agatha breezes past her, heels clicking against the tile, then pauses at the threshold when Rio doesn’t follow. “Why are you still standing there? Come on. The place closes at ten.”

Rio just stares, tongue pressed to the inside of her cheek. 

“Agatha, are you asking me out on a date?”

Her boss looks at her, a little taken aback, then actually laughs. 

“Sometimes I forget how ridiculous and blunt the things are that come out of your mouth.” Agatha rolls her eyes as Rio’s grin only widens, that signature gap between her teeth on full display. “But no, it’s not a date. I’m simply rewarding my infuriatingly competent assistant for a job well done this week.” She waves a hand, already turning away. “Now wipe that smug little grin off your face and meet me in the car.”

The restaurant is set al fresco, right by the river, with the city lights dancing on the water like tiny stars. 

It’s obvious Agatha comes here often from the way the staff greet her like they know her well, already leading them to her usual table and reciting her usual order without being asked. 

The air smells of fresh herbs and grilled seafood, and Rio can faintly hear traffic from the bridge nearby. 

It basically feels like New York pretending to be Paris.

“Okay, so this was peak stupid,” Rio grins as she spears a piece of shrimp. Agatha had just asked her about a fond memory growing up. “Alice and I were like nine, maybe ten. She had this janky old pink Barbie Jeep, one of those Power Wheels that were all the rage back then? She begged Lorna for it for Christmas. And for some reason, our idiot friends thought it’d be genius to duct-tape Roman candles to the back. Like we were turning it into a damn rocket or something.”

Agatha slowly sets her glass down. “And let me guess, you thought it was genius too.”

“Hey, I was a kid obsessed with outer space. Of course I thought it was genius,” she laughs, then leans in a little, evidently enjoying herself. “So there I was, in the driver’s seat of this pink Barbie Jeep, goggles on, Alice riding shotgun, and the plan was she’d light the Roman candles while I drove.”

Agatha tries not to look too entertained. “And did this plan of yours actually work?”

“Oh, it worked, alright,” Rio says proudly, laughing so hard she can barely get the words out. “For seven glorious seconds, we were flying down the street in this pink, plastic firework chariot. The most beautiful kind of madness a ten-year-old could ever dream of.”

Agatha simply looks at her fondly, and Rio isn’t sure what to do with it so she just keeps talking. “But then the battery dies halfway. The car just stops, completely dead, right in front of Mr. Miller’s yard, which was conveniently covered in a giant pile of dry leaves.”

“Oh god, you didn’t?” Agatha says, smiling now like she already knows where the story’s going. 

Rio grins, shrugging. “The Roman candle sparks shot straight into the pile of leaves.”

Agatha gapes at her, laughing. “You little arsonist.”

“Well, to be fair, it wasn’t even that big. It was just a baby fire,” Rio says through a laugh. “But unfortunately, it was still enough to send Mr. Miller sprinting out of his house in slippers and a fishing hat, screaming bloody murder and calling the Delaware Fire Department.”

And her boss actually laughs out loud. “And somehow, you both still made it to adulthood.”

Rio rests her chin in her hand, still smiling. “Barely. We were grounded for two weeks and banned from anything that plugs in or uses batteries. But totally worth it. That Barbie Jeep really looked badass.”

Agatha shakes her head, smiling despite herself, then watches Rio for a beat longer.

“Do you miss it?”

“The Barbie Jeep?” Rio grins.

Agatha snorts and rolls her eyes, but Rio catches the softness in it, and knows exactly what she meant. Her own smile lingers as she leans back, eyes drifting to her glass.

“I mean…yeah, sometimes. Delaware’s always gonna be home in a way,” she answers with a small shrug. She pauses, then adds, “I’m actually planning to go visit them in a few weeks. It would’ve been their 25th anniversary. And I’ve got a feeling they’d straight-up haunt my ass if I didn’t show.”

A waitress then approaches their table with two beautifully plated desserts, and Rio looks between them, realizing hers is different from what Agatha is having. 

Her boss seems to catch the look and says, “I figured you’d like that one better, since you always scrape off the frosting on those cheap cupcakes you bring to the office, and you barely touched that caramel drink Javier got you the other day.”

Rio doesn’t respond right away, caught off guard once again by the fact that Agatha had noticed something so small about her. But instead of sitting with that realization, and noticing that Agatha doesn’t seem to think much of what she just said, she blurts out the first thing that comes to mind.

“Alright, enough about me. What about you? Any fond childhood memories?” 

Agatha raises a brow, as if the idea itself is foreign. She takes a slow sip of her wine before replying, “Nothing quite as exciting as yours. I was homeschooled most of my life, so I didn’t really grow up with friends. The closest thing I had were our old neighbors and a pet rabbit my father gave me. Probably just to shut me up about wanting to go to an actual school.” 

Rio tilts her head. “Wait, seriously?”

“I was homeschooled until high school by this old hag named Mrs. Parker who used to smack my wrist with a ruler if I so much as slouched,” Agatha says simply, and Rio just looks at her, not sure what to say. “I was mostly on my own. Sometimes my parents had friends over, and they’d bring their kids, but let’s just say I didn’t exactly make the best impression to earn their friendship.”

“And why’s that?”

Agatha shrugs, taking another slow sip of her wine. “I once put gum and a beetle in one of their daughters’ hair after she called me a freak for not going to school like the rest of them.”

Rio chokes on her drink, trying not to laugh. “That’s diabolical. And kind of sad.”

“Well, I beg to differ,” her boss replies dryly. “Because that was when my mother finally decided I needed proper socialization. So she threw me into an actual school. Catholic, of course, but at least it got me out of the house. And it also happened to be where I realized I really, really like girls. So I guess I should be thanking my dear mother for that.”

As Rio laughs into her drink, a voice speaks from behind them.

“Agatha?” 

They both turn, and Rio looks up at an older woman in pearls and a cream shawl, clutching what looks like a very expensive handbag. She appears to be in her late seventies, with perfectly combed hair and a face pulled so tight Rio could almost count the Botox injections.

Agatha sits up a little straighter. “Mrs. Davis.”

“My goodness, it’s been ages,” the woman coos. “I almost didn’t recognize you. My, how you’ve grown. The last time I saw you, you were practically glued to your nanny’s hip at every garden party. How are you, dear?” But before Agatha can answer, the woman barrels on. “I heard your cousin’s wedding is next week. She’s marrying that sweet rich boy from Monaco, yes? Will you be attending?”

“I’m still working through my schedule,” Agatha replies, though Rio knows her boss hasn’t even thought about clearing her calendar for it next week. “Running a magazine keeps me pretty busy.”

“Oh, just like your father. Always so driven and all business. My Lisa recently tied the knot, too. Lovely ceremony. Good young man, and very well-off,” she says with a self-satisfied chuckle. “They’re already talking about babies. You know, women your age aren’t getting any younger. I always wondered when you’d settle down with a nice man yourself. Your mother wouldn’t say a word when asked about you. She just tells us you’re too busy to even find a man. Or maybe you already have?”

Agatha doesn’t blink. “No, I haven’t found a man,” she says, raising her glass. “And I manage just fine without. But please give Lisa and her husband my best. I’m sure there’ll be grandkids soon enough to wear down your already fragile hips. If they haven’t started already.” She sips her drink slowly, still smiling, as if she’d just paid the woman a compliment.

Rio nearly chokes on her drink trying not to laugh, but the stern look Mrs. Davis throws her way says she didn’t quite pull it off. The older woman straightens her shoulders, visibly trying to recover her dignity as she turns back to Agatha. 

“Still as spirited as ever, I see. Well, I won’t keep you. My husband’s waiting in the car.” Her eyes pass over Agatha, then linger on Rio a little longer, her courteous smile barely concealing the judgment behind it, before returning to the other woman. “It was really lovely seeing you, dear. I’ll let your mother know you’re…doing quite well.” She throws one last loaded look to the both of them, then turns and walks away.

Rio watches the old woman reach the doorway, a sarcastic comment already forming in her head. But when she turns back, the words instantly disappear, because Agatha is already looking at her. And if Rio is not imagining it, there’s something in her eyes. A little worry. Maybe even a bit of fear.

“Hey, are you okay?” She places her hand on top of Agatha’s without really thinking. 

The touch seems to snap Agatha out of whatever thought she was lost in. She looks at their hands for a moment, then quickly pulls hers away.

“I’m fine,” she clears her throat and takes one last sip of her drink. “But I think we should call it a night. I just remembered I’ve got an early weekend tomorrow. And also, all this wine is starting to get to me.”

Before Rio can say anything, Agatha raises a hand to get the waiter’s attention. 

“Simon. The check, please.”

The ride to Rio’s apartment is thick with silence. She’d wanted to take the subway, but Agatha insisted on driving her and made sure Javier was okay with it. Javier, of course, said he didn’t mind. 

Rio has no clue why the mood between her and Agatha turned so sharply after that awkward run-in with one of her mother’s high-society cronies. They’d been laughing and talking just moments before, and now it feels like someone hit a lever, and Agatha’s icy wall slides right back up into place. 

Guess even fever dreams have a bedtime.

Rio looks across at her, who is pointedly staring out the window as the city lights blur by. Then her eyes drop to the small space between them, their fingers just inches apart. She knows Agatha feels it too.

She stays still for a few more seconds, staring straight ahead and weighing the consequences of what she’s about to do. 

But then, perhaps fueled by a late surge of courage from all the wine she’d had earlier, she takes a deep breath and slowly hooks her pinky around Agatha’s. 

Their fingers touch for a brief second before Agatha moves her hand, flicking a strand of hair behind her ear. 

“I almost forgot,” she breaks the silence as well as Rio’s fragile rush of bravery. “Tell Jen I won’t be coming in next week. Have her cancel all my personal meetings, and move the important ones online if you can.”

Rio stares at her briefly, then pulls out her phone. 

“You’re going to your cousin’s wedding?”

She doesn’t even try to hide her surprise, especially since Agatha never had any plans to begin with, and has also been deliberately ignoring every call from Morris County that’s come into the office since that last conversation with her mother months ago.

Agatha answers the question with another instruction. 

“Also, tell Sue and Monica to send a few dresses and suits to my place tomorrow morning. One for the wedding, obviously. One for the rehearsal dinner, and two more in case my family throws any last-minute crappy get-togethers. And it’s going to be a garden wedding, so nothing floral and absolutely no frills. If they so much as send me anything with ruffles, I’ll strangle them with it myself.”

The first three days of Agatha being out of the office feel like a peculiar kind of withdrawal for Rio. 

She keeps finding her eyes drifting to the glass walls of her boss’s office, half-expecting to catch her mid-rant on a call or hammering away at her keyboard. But, of course, the room stays empty.

The hours drag on, and Rio feels oddly useless since Jen’s getting most of the calls and direct orders from their boss, who seems to be ignoring her again on purpose. Rio hates that all she can do is stupidly perk up every time she overhears a “Yes, Agatha. I’ll get to it,” or an “Okay, Agatha. I’ll let them know.”

The fifth day drags on like the rest, but Rio snaps out of her funk when she hears Jen sneezing, sniffling, and coughing nonstop while juggling papers and phone calls. At one point, unable to take the awful sounds anymore, Rio hands her a tissue. “How’s that cold holding up?” she asks.

Jen snorts. “Like I’m already dead.” She drags her hands to her face. “God, I can’t afford to be sick. Especially now. There’s still so much to get done here, and Agatha expects...” She trails off as another sneeze interrupts her. Rio hands her the whole box of tissues. “Thanks,” Jen mutters, wiping her nose.
